Zakah to the Father and Wife of a Rich Person
Intra-Hanafi dispute on giving zakah to the father and wife of a rich person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on giving zakah to the father and wife of a rich person, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the wife's eligibility.

Core Idea
A rich person's father may receive zakah if poor; his wife may receive it if she lacks accessible wealth—the relied view permits it.

This lesson addresses two specific categories of zakah recipients: the father of a wealthy person and the wife of a wealthy person. While the father's case is straightforward, the wife's eligibility involves a known intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute clarifies when zakat can be given to a wife who has no dower or inaccessible dower from her husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage discusses two separate cases: (1) the father of a rich person, and (2) the wife of a rich person. Note that the father's ruling is given without dispute, while the wife's ruling involves ikhtilaf.
The father must be poor to receive zakah; this is agreed upon.
2
Analyze the wife's case without dower
The passage presents three views: Abu Yusuf says she is not given, Muhammad says she is given, and a report in al-Karkhi says both agree she is given. The text then states the correct view is qawluhuma (the view of the two, i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agreeing she is given).
The pronoun 'qawluhuma' here refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, not the two Sahibayn? Actually in Hanafi texts, 'qawluhuma' often refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. But the passage mentions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad separately, then says 'al-ashah qawluhuma' meaning the correct view is their joint view (that she is given).
3
Consider the wife with a dower but insolvent husband
If the wife has a dower of two hundred dirhams but her husband is insolvent (cannot pay), she may still receive zakah because her wealth is not immediately accessible. This shows that the criterion is actual accessibility of wealth, not mere ownership.
The phrase 'إن كان معسراً' means if the husband is insolvent, i.e., unable to pay the dower.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text explicitly says 'al-ashah qawluhuma', meaning the relied-upon view is that the wife is given zakah. This resolves the dispute in favor of permissibility.
This is an example of tarjih within the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a wife of a wealthy man has no independent wealth (no dower or inaccessible dower), she qualifies as a recipient of zakah. The payer should check her financial situation, not her husband's.
This ruling is based on the principle that the husband's wealth is not the wife's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أبو الغني: فيجوز دفع الزكاة إليه إذا كان فقيراً. وأما زوجة الغني إذا لم يكن لها على زوجها مهر : قال بعضهم: وقال في المنتقى»: لا تُعطى عند أبي يوسف، وتعطى عند محمد. وفي «الكرخي»: تُعطى عندهما. وقال أبو يوسف : لا تُعطى، والأصح قولهما. وإن كان لها مهر يبلغ مائتي درهم: إن كان معسراً: يجوز لها الأخذ، وللدافع: الإعطاء.
As for the father of a rich person, it is permissible to pay zakah to him if he is poor. As for the wife of a rich person when she has no dower from her husband: some said, and in al-Muntaqa: she is not given according to Abu Yusuf, and is given according to Muhammad. And in al-Karkhi: she is given according to both. And Abu Yusuf said: she is not given, and the correct view is the view of the two (that she is given). And if she has a dower that reaches two hundred dirhams, if he (the husband) is insolvent, it is permissible for her to take and for the payer to give.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage covers two more categories: the father and the wife of a rich person. The father of a rich person, if he is poor, may receive zakah without dispute. The wife of a rich person, however, is subject to ikhtilaf. If she has no dower (mahr) from her husband, Abu Yusuf says she should not be given zakah, while Muhammad says she should. In al-Karkhi, it is reported that both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree she is given. The text then states the correct view (al-ashah) is the view of the two (likely referring to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that she is given). Additionally, if she has a dower of two hundred dirhams but her husband is insolvent (cannot pay), she may still receive zakah because her wealth is not immediately accessible.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift (dower) that the husband gives to the wife upon marriage, which becomes her property.
معسر (mu'sir): Insolvent; a person who is unable to pay his debts or fulfill financial obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied upon view in this passage, what is the ruling on giving zakah to the wife of a rich man who has no dower from her husband?
A) She is not given zakah
B) She is given zakah
C) She is given only if her husband is poor
D) She is not given unless she is divorced

Key Takeaway
The relied Hanafi view allows giving zakah to the wife of a wealthy man if she lacks accessible wealth, as her need is evaluated independently.

Giving Zakat to an Adult Poor Child of a Wealthy Parent
Rulings on zakat for adult poor children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to an adult poor son or daughter and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the timing of maintenance.

Core Idea
An adult poor child is not wealthy by the father's wealth, but timing of maintenance matters for permissibility.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a specific scenario: can zakat be given to an adult poor child of a wealthy parent? While the child is poor in their own right, the father may be obligated to support them. The Hanafi school has an internal difference on whether the imposition of maintenance affects the permissibility of giving zakat.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify that giving zakat to an adult poor child is fundamentally permissible because the child is not considered wealthy by the parent's wealth.
This applies whether the child is healthy or disabled, but note the distinction.
2
Identify the condition
Check if the child is disabled (zamin) – then maintenance is obligatory on the father. This triggers the dispute.
Maintenance is fard only if the child is unable to work.
3
Note the consensus
Before the father's maintenance is fixed by a judge or agreement, there is consensus on permissibility.
This is because the child is still seen as needy.
4
Explore the dispute
After maintenance is fixed, Muhammad permits because the maintenance is not wealth in hand; Abu Yusuf prohibits because the child's need is covered.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf rooted in the nature of maintenance as wealth.
5
Apply to the daughter
The same ruling applies to the adult daughter, but the text mentions a more correct view that she should not be given, as she is considered wealthy through her father and husband.
Some scholars said it is permissible; others said not; the more correct is the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف ما إذا كان كبيراً فقيراً: فإنه يجوز الدفع إليه؛ لأنه لا يُعدُّ غنياً بيسار أبيه ولو كانت نفقته عليه، بأن كان زمناً.
وقيل: إن كان زَمِناً: يجوز الدفع إليه قبل أن تفرض نفقته على أبيه، بالإجماع، وبعد الفرض: يجوز عند محمد ؛ لأنه لا يعد غنياً بمقدار النفقة.
وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز بعد الفرض.
وهكذا حكم البنت الكبيرة.
- وفي «الفتاوى» : إذا دفع إلى ابنة الغني الكبيرة:
يصير
قال بعضهم: يجوز؛ لأنها لا تُعَدُّ غنيةً بغنى أبيها وزوجها، وقال بعضهم: لا يجوز، وهو الأصح.
تعطى.
وأما أبو الغني: فيجوز دفع الزكاة إليه إذا كان فقيراً.
وأما زوجة الغني إذا لم يكن لها على زوجها مهر : قال بعضهم:
وقال في المنتقى»: لا تُعطى عند أبي يوسف، وتعطى عند محمد.
وفي «الكرخي»: تُعطى عندهما.
وقال أبو يوسف : لا تُعطى، والأصح قولهما.
وإن كان لها مهر يبلغ مائتي درهم: إن كان معسراً: يجوز لها الأخذ، وللدافع: الإعطاء.
وإن كان موسراً: فكذلك يجوز أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة.
وعندهما: لا يجوز؛ بناء على أن المهر في الذمة ليس بنصاب عنده، وعندهما هو نصاب.
Unlike the case if he is an adult poor person: then it is permissible to give to him; because he is not considered wealthy by the wealth of his father, even if his maintenance is obligatory upon his father, if he is disabled. And it was said: If he is disabled, it is permissible to give to him before his maintenance is made obligatory on his father by consensus, and after it is made obligatory, it is permissible according to Muhammad, because he is not wealthy by the amount of maintenance. Abu Yusuf said: It is not permissible after it is made obligatory. Similarly is the ruling for the adult daughter. In al-Fatawa: If one gives to the adult daughter of a wealthy man: some said it is permissible; because she is not considered wealthy by the wealth of her father and husband, and some said it is not permissible, and that is the more correct. She is given. As for the father of a wealthy man: it is permissible to give zakat to him if he is poor. As for the wife of a wealthy man if she does not have a dowry from her husband: some said: and in al-Muntaqa: it is not given according to Abu Yusuf, and is given according to Muhammad. In al-Karkhi: it is given according to both. Abu Yusuf said: it is not given, and the more correct is their statement. And if she has a dowry that reaches two hundred dirhams: if the husband is insolvent: it is permissible for her to take, and for the giver to give. And if he is wealthy: then it is also permissible according to Abu Hanifa. According to them both: it is not permissible; based on the fact that a dowry in the debt is not a nisab according to him, while according to them it is a nisab.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira discusses the conditions under which zakat may be given to certain needy relatives of a wealthy person. It is a commentary expanding on the matn of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The passage begins by contrasting the case of a minor child (not mentioned here) with that of an adult poor son: giving zakat to an adult poor son is permissible because he is not considered wealthy by his father's wealth, even if his father is obligated to support him. If the son is disabled, there is a consensus that it is permissible before the father's maintenance obligation is formally imposed; after imposition, Muhammad permits it while Abu Yusuf does not. The same ruling applies to an adult daughter, with additional opinions in al-Fatawa that the more correct view is that it is not permissible to give to her, as she is deemed wealthy through her father and husband. As for the poor father of a wealthy man, giving zakat to him is permissible. Regarding the wife of a wealthy man without a dowry, there is disagreement: according to the relied-upon position, she may be given zakat. If she has a dowry of two hundred dirhams (nisab), Abu Hanifa allows giving zakat to her even if her husband is wealthy, because the dowry in the debt is not considered a nisab according to him. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it a nisab and thus disallow giving zakat to her. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights different understandings of wealth and nisab in the context of debts.

Key Terms
فقير (faqir): A person who does not possess nisab and is eligible for zakat
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, the financial support a father owes to his children in certain conditions
زمن (zamin): Disabled, physically unable to earn

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on giving zakat to an adult disabled son after the father's maintenance is made obligatory?
A) Permissible by consensus
B) Permissible according to Muhammad, not according to Abu Yusuf
C) Not permissible by consensus
D) Permissible only if the son is healthy

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat to an adult poor child is generally permissible, but after maintenance is fixed, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad permits, Abu Yusuf prohibits.

Giving Zakat to the Poor Wife of a Wealthy Husband
Rulings on zakat for the wife of a wealthy man

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi dispute regarding giving zakat to a poor wife and determine the mufta bihi position based on whether she has a dowry.

Core Idea
A wife's eligibility for zakat depends on whether her husband's debt (mahr) is considered wealth, causing a split between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

The second part of the passage deals with giving zakat to the wife of a wealthy man. If she has no dowry, there is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; the relied-upon view is that she may receive zakat. If she has a dowry of nisab amount, Abu Hanifa permits giving zakat because the mahr is a debt not in hand, while the Sahibayn consider it wealth and disallow it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case – wife without mahr
Start with the simpler case: the wife is poor and has no dowry owed by her husband. Determine if she can receive zakat.
The husband is wealthy, but she is personally poor.
2
Note the positions
Muhammad permits giving zakat; Abu Yusuf prohibits. The text states the more correct view (al-ashah) is the statement of the Sahibayn (both allowing it).
This means the mufta bihi is that she can receive zakat.
3
Discuss the second case – wife with mahr of nisab
If the wife has a dowry owed to her that reaches nisab (200 dirhams), her eligibility depends on whether that debt is considered wealth.
The husband may be solvent (muyassar) or insolvent (mu'sir).
4
Analyze the usul point
Abu Hanifa: a debt (mahr) in the husband's liability is not considered a nisab because it is not in hand, so she is still poor. The Sahibayn: the debt is a valuable asset and constitutes nisab, so she cannot receive zakat.
This is a fundamental difference in the concept of wealth (ghina) regarding receivables.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text gives positions but does not explicitly state a tarjih for the mahr case. However, by noting that for the no-mahr case the more correct is the Sahibayn, and that Abu Hanifa's view is presented without endorsement, it suggests the Sahibayn's view is likely stronger.
Later Hanafis often side with the Sahibayn in this issue.

It is a commentary expanding on the matn of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The passage begins by contrasting the case of a minor child (not mentioned here) with that of an adult poor son: giving zakat to an adult poor son is permissible because he is not considered wealthy by his father's wealth, even if his father is obligated to support him. If the son is disabled, there is a consensus that it is permissible before the father's maintenance obligation is formally imposed; after imposition, Muhammad permits it while Abu Yusuf does not. The same ruling applies to an adult daughter, with additional opinions in al-Fatawa that the more correct view is that it is not permissible to give to her, as she is deemed wealthy through her father and husband. As for the poor father of a wealthy man, giving zakat to him is permissible. Regarding the wife of a wealthy man without a dowry, there is disagreement: according to the relied-upon position, she may be given zakat. If she has a dowry of two hundred dirhams (nisab), Abu Hanifa allows giving zakat to her even if her husband is wealthy, because the dowry in the debt is not considered a nisab according to him. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it a nisab and thus disallow giving zakat to her. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights different understandings of wealth and nisab in the context of debts.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The mandatory bridal gift from husband to wife in marriage
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Legal liability, obligation in the abstract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the more correct view regarding giving zakat to a wife who has no dowry?
A) She should not be given, according to Abu Yusuf
B) She should be given, according to Muhammad
C) She should be given, and this is the more correct view
D) It is not permissible in any case

Key Takeaway
A wife's eligibility for zakat depends on whether an owed mahr is considered wealth; Abu Hanifa permits giving zakat to her if she has a mahr of nisab, while the Sahibayn do not, and for a wife without mahr, the relied-upon view is that she may receive zakat.

General Rule for Sadaqat Recipients and Exception for Treasure
General rule and exception for treasure and mines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for recipients of various obligatory charities and identify the exception for treasure and mines, explaining the Hanafi qiyas reasoning.

Core Idea
Treasure khums is personal property; giving it to parents is permissible unlike zakat.

This passage establishes a foundational rule in Hanafi fiqh: the same categories of recipients apply to zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, vows, expiations, and tithes. However, an important exception is made for treasure troves and mines. Understanding this exception reveals a key Hanafi principle about the flexible nature of treasure khums compared to regular sadaqat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The author states that all the recipients previously mentioned for zakat apply equally to other obligatory charities: sadaqat al-fitr, vows, expiations, and tithes. This is the default rule.
The general rule aims to unify the recipient categories across multiple forms of charity.
2
Spot the Exception
Treasures and mines are expressly excluded from the general rule. For these, the khums (one-fifth) may be given to parents, husband, and wife—close relatives who are normally prohibited from receiving zakat.
The exception is for 'kunuz' and 'ma'adin' specifically.
3
Analyze the Proof (Qiyas)
The proof is a qiyas argument: if the finder of treasure is allowed to keep the khums for himself when the four-fifths are insufficient, then giving it to his close relatives is even more appropriate (awla). This is a 'qiyas al-awla' (argument from priority).
The Arabic text: 'لأنه يجوز أن يحبسه لنفسه ... فإذا جاز لنفسه: فغيره أولى'
4
Understand the Practical Implication
In practice, this ruling means that treasure khums can support one's immediate family, unlike zakat which generally cannot be given to ascendants or descendants. This flexibility is due to the personal ownership nature of treasure.
The ruling is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) according to later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب من يجوز دفع الصدقة إليه، ومن لا يجوز
وجميع ما ذكرنا من المصارف حكمهم سواء في الزكاة، وصدقة الفطر، والنذور، والكفارات، والعشور، إلا في الكنوز والمعادن خاصةً، فإن خُمس ذلك يجوز صرفه إلى الوالدين والزوج والزوجة؛ لأنه يجوز أن يحبسه لنفسه إذا كانت الأربعة الأخماس لا تكفيه، فإذا جاز لنفسه: فغيره أولى.
Chapter: On who it is permissible to give charity to and who it is not. And all that we have mentioned of recipients, their ruling is the same regarding zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, vows, expiations, and tithes, except for treasures and mines specifically, for indeed the fifth of that is permissible to be given to parents, husband, and wife; because it is permissible for him to keep it for himself if the four-fifths are not sufficient for him, so if it is permissible for himself, then for others it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (bab) on who may receive sadaqah. The author states that the rules for recipients previously discussed for zakat apply equally to other obligatory charities: sadaqat al-fitr (zakah al-fitr), nadhr (vows), kaffarah (expiation), and ushr (tithe on agricultural produce). However, there is an exception for treasure troves (kunuz) and mines (ma'adin). For these, the one-fifth due (khums) may be given to one's parents, spouse, and children? The text says parents, husband, and wife (al-walidayn wal-zawj wal-zawjah). The reasoning is that if the finder of treasure keeps the four-fifths for himself, and if that four-fifths is insufficient for his needs, he may even use the fifth for himself. Since it is permissible for him, it is even more appropriate to give it to his close relatives. This is a qiyas-based proof (argument from priority). The ruling for treasure is thus more flexible than regular zakat, where giving to ascendants and descendants is generally not allowed.

Key Terms
كنز (kanz): Treasure trove; buried wealth from pre-Islamic or unknown ownership
معدن (ma'dan): Mine; extracted minerals on which khums is due

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat al-fitr can be given to anyone
B) Khums of treasure can be given to parents and spouse
C) Vows can be given to non-Muslims
D) There is no exception

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows giving treasure khums to parents and spouse, unlike regular zakat, based on a qiyas argument of priority.

Tamlik vs. Ibaha in Zakat: Three Intra-Hanafi Cases
Three case rulings on zakat with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and ibaha in zakat distribution and identify the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on feeding relatives/orphans from zakat.

Core Idea
Zakat requires ownership transfer (tamlik); mere permission to use (ibaha) is insufficient.

This passage from al-Fatawa presents three specific cases illustrating a critical Hanafi principle: zakat must be given as tamlik (ownership transfer), not merely as ibaha (permission to consume). An intra-school disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani reveals the practical implications of this principle. Let's examine each case to understand the ruling and its basis.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Principle
Recognize that tamlik (ownership transfer) is a necessary condition for a valid zakat payment. Ibaha (permission to use) does not fulfill the obligation.
2
Identify the First Case and Intra-Madhhab Positions
Case: a man gives clothing and food to a brother under maintenance order, intending zakat. Note Abu Yusuf allows both; Muhammad allows clothing but not food.
Muhammad's reasoning: food is ibaha, clothing is tamlik (the brother owns the clothes).
3
Analyze the Reasoning for the Ikhtilaf
Why does Muhammad prohibit food? Because feeding is ibaha – the recipient merely has permission to eat, not ownership. Zakat requires the recipient to own the amount. Abu Yusuf considers feeding a valid discharge.
The exception: if the food is given 'into his hand' it becomes tamlik.
4
Apply the Principle to the Orphan and Feeding Back Cases
Orphan case: clothing from zakat is valid, but food is not unless given into the orphan's hand (tamlik). Feeding back: if a poor person feeds the giver after receiving zakat, it is invalid because it reverts to ibaha.
The 'feeding back' case shows that once zakat is given as tamlik, the poor person owns it and may feed the giver only if done as tamlik (e.g., as a gift).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: following Muhammad, feeding a relative from zakat does not count; following Abu Yusuf, it does. The text hints at Muhammad's view being stronger by noting the exception of 'into his hand'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في «الفتاوى»: رجلٌ له أخ قضى القاضي عليه بنفقته، فكساه وأطعمه ينوي به الزكاة
فعند أبي يوسف: يجوز فيهما، وعند محمد: يجوز في الكسوة، ولا يجوز في الإطعام.
ومن عال يتيماً بكسوة، وبنفقة من الزكاة: جاز في الكسوة، دون الإطعام؛ لأنه في الإطعام: إباحة، إلا أن يدفع إلى يده.
وعن أبي يوسف: يجوز فيهما.
- رجل أعطى فقيراً من زكاته أو من عُشر أرضه، أو من فطرته، ثم
إن الفقير أطعمه المعطي: لا يجوز ذلك، إلا على سبيل التمليك، ولا يجوز على سبيل الإباحة.
It is said in al-Fatawa: A man has a brother for whom the judge decreed maintenance upon him, so he clothed him and fed him intending thereby zakat. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible in both. According to Muhammad, it is permissible in clothing and not permissible in feeding. And whoever supports an orphan with clothing and maintenance from zakat: it is permissible regarding clothing, not regarding feeding; because feeding is ibaha (permission to use), unless he gives it into his hand. And from Abu Yusuf: it is permissible in both. A man gave a poor person from his zakat or from the ushr of his land or from his fitr, then the poor person fed the giver: that is not permissible, except on the basis of tamlik (ownership), and it is not permissible on the basis of ibaha.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents three specific scenarios from al-Fatawa regarding the payment of zakat. First, a man has a brother for whom a judge has ordered maintenance (nafaqah). If he gives clothing and food to that brother intending zakat, Abu Yusuf permits both, but Muhammad permits only clothing, not food. The reasoning for Muhammad is that feeding is ibaha (a permission to eat, not ownership), and zakat requires tamlik (transfer of ownership). Secondly, supporting an orphan with zakat: clothing is allowed, but food is not unless the orphan is given ownership of the food (if it is placed in his hand). Again, ibaha is insufficient. Abu Yusuf, however, permits both. Thirdly, a general rule: if a person gives zakat, ushr, or fitr to a poor person, and that poor person then feeds the giver, it is not valid because the poor person must own the payment; feeding back is ibaha, not tamlik. The exception is if the poor person explicitly gives it as ownership (tamlik) to the giver. This emphasizes the Hanafi principle that zakat must be given as tamlik (ownership), not as mere permission to use.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibaha): Permission to use or consume without transfer of ownership
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; a necessary condition for valid zakat payment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibit giving zakat as food to a brother under maintenance order?
A) Because the brother is wealthy
B) Because food is ibaha, not tamlik
C) Because the judge's order forbids it
D) Because it creates a family conflict

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given as tamlik (ownership transfer); feeding a person (ibaha) does not discharge the obligation unless the food is given into the recipient's hand as ownership.

Is a Mahr Debt Considered Nisab for Zakat?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr debt as nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a mahr debt constitutes nisab for zakat eligibility and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, a mahr debt is not nisab; for the Sahibayn, it is. The school relies on the Sahibayn's view.

This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school about the definition of wealth for zakat. The specific issue is whether a mahr (dower) that is owed as a debt but not yet received should count toward the nisab threshold. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the school's operative ruling in contemporary zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify that Abu Hanifa considers a mahr in dhimma as not constituting nisab. Therefore, a person whose only wealth is such a debt is eligible to receive zakat.
This is the exception within the school.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they treat a mahr debt as equivalent to wealth in hand, so it counts toward nisab, making the person ineligible for zakat.
Their view is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).
3
Trace the basis of the ikhtilaf
The difference hinges on whether a debt receivable (dayn) is treated as present wealth for nisab calculation. Abu Hanifa: not equivalent; Sahibayn: equivalent.
No explicit proof is given in the passage; the dispute is talil (reasoning-based).
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: whether zakat can be given to someone whose only asset is a mahr claim of less than nisab. According to the Sahibayn, it cannot.
Fatwa follows the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان موسراً: فكذلك يجوز أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما: لا يجوز؛ بناء على أن المهر في الذمة ليس بنصاب عنده، وعندهما هو نصاب.
And if he is wealthy: then similarly it is permissible also according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is not permissible; based on the fact that the dower (mahr) in the debt is not a nisab according to him, whereas according to the two it is a nisab.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that a person whose only wealth is a mahr receivable as a debt is not considered wealthy because that debt is not a complete nisab (the minimum threshold for zakat eligibility). Therefore, it is permissible to give them zakat. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider the mahr debt as part of the nisab since it is a recoverable asset, thus the person is wealthy and ineligible for zakat. The dispute hinges on the legal characterization of a debt (dayn) in one's favor.

Key Terms
المهر في الذمة (al-mahr fi al-dhimma): A dower that is owed as a debt, not yet received.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum threshold of wealth requiring zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling regarding giving zakat to a person whose only wealth is a mahr debt of 150 dirhams?
A) Permissible, because the debt is less than nisab.
B) Not permissible, because the debt is considered part of nisab.
C) Permissible, because the person has not received the money.
D) Not permissible, because mahr is not eligible for zakat.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a recoverable mahr debt counts toward zakat nisab, so giving zakat to such a person is not permissible according to the relied-upon position.

General Rule for Zakat Recipients and Exception for Khumus of Treasures and Mines
Listing zakat recipients and exception for khumus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of recipients for various sadaqat and state the Hanafi exception for the khumus of treasures and mines, explaining the rationale.

Core Idea
The khumus of treasures and mines may be given to parents and spouse, unlike other sadaqat.

This passage establishes a general principle: the categories of recipients for zakat, zakat al-fitr, vows, expiations, and 'ushur are identical. However, there is a notable exception for the one-fifth (khumus) extracted from buried treasures and mines. Understanding this exception is crucial for proper distribution of these funds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
State that the categories of recipients (the eight asnaf) apply uniformly to zakat, zakat al-fitr, vows, expiations, and tithes. This is the default ruling.
The eight categories are: poor, needy, collectors, reconciling hearts, freeing slaves, debtors, in the path of Allah, and the wayfarer.
2
Identify the Exception
Note that the khumus of treasures and mines is an exception. Its ruling differs from the other sadaqat: it may be given to parents, husband, and wife.
This exception applies only to khumus, not to the four-fifths share.
3
Understand the Rationale
Analyze the reasoning: the owner is permitted to keep the khumus for himself if the four-fifths are insufficient for his needs. Since he can benefit himself, giving to close relatives is even more appropriate.
This is based on qiyas (analogy) with the permissibility of spending on oneself.
4
Apply the Ruling
Practice applying the rule: in a case where a person finds a buried treasure, he gives four-fifths to the state (or appropriate authority) and may distribute the remaining one-fifth to his parents, spouse, or children, or keep it if he is in need.
This is a practical application of the exception.

Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage states a general rule: the categories of recipients for zakat, zakat al-fitr, nadhr (vows), kaffarat (expiations), and 'ushur (tithes) are the same. However, there is an exception for the khumus (one-fifth) of treasures (kanuz) and mines (ma'adin). That khumus may be given to one's parents, spouse, and children (husband/wife) because the owner is allowed to keep the four-fifths for himself if needed. Since he can benefit himself, giving to close relatives is even more appropriate.

Zakat by Feeding vs. Clothing: The Tamlik Requirement
Fatwa on zakat via feeding clothing/food to a brother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on zakat via feeding, and apply the Hanafi principle that zakat requires tamlik (ownership transfer) to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
Zakat requires tamlik; feeding is ibahah unless food is given as property.

Zakat is an act of worship that must fulfill specific legal conditions. One crucial condition is that the recipient must receive full ownership (tamlik) of the zakat asset. This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about whether feeding a person with zakat intention satisfies this condition. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani both agree on the principle, but differ on whether a meal constitutes tamlik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man is legally required to support his brother. He gives him clothing and food, intending these as zakat. The fatwa considers each item separately.
The court order does not affect the zakat ruling; the donor's intention matters.
2
State the two positions
Abu Yusuf: both clothing and food are valid zakat. Muhammad: only clothing is valid; food is not.
3
Trace the basis of each view
Both imams agree on the tamlik requirement. They differ on whether a meal transfers ownership. Muhammad views feeding as ibahah (permission to eat), not tamlik. Abu Yusuf sees it as a sufficient form of tamlik because the recipient owns the food once consumed.
Muhammad's reasoning: the food is consumed immediately, so ownership is not established beforehand.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If you invite a poor person to a meal intending zakat, according to Muhammad that meal does not count. According to Abu Yusuf it does. Similarly, giving a cooked dish as a takeaway may be treated differently from eating on the spot.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa practice favors Abu Yusuf's view as more practical and lenient, allowing both clothing and food.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin, not zahir riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في «الفتاوى»: رجلٌ له أخ قضى القاضي عليه بنفقته، فكساه وأطعمه ينوي به الزكاة فعند أبي يوسف: يجوز فيهما، وعند محمد: يجوز في الكسوة، ولا يجوز في الإطعام.
It is stated in al-Fatawa: A man has a brother for whom the judge has ordered him to provide maintenance; he clothed him and fed him intending thereby zakat. According to Abu Yusuf: it is permissible in both. According to Muhammad: it is permissible in clothing, but not in feeding.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This fatwa addresses a scenario where a man is legally obligated to support his brother. He gives him clothing and food with the intention of discharging his zakat. Abu Yusuf holds that both clothing and food are permissible as zakat because they fulfill a need and ownership is transferred. Muhammad distinguishes: clothing is permissible because it is a tangible asset that can be owned, while food given as a meal is merely an ibahah (permission to use) and not full ownership, so it does not count as zakat. This reflects the Hanafi principle that zakat must transfer ownership (tamlik), not just benefit.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-it'am): Feeding someone, which may be ibahah (permission to eat) rather than tamlik (ownership).
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership – a necessary condition for zakat in the Hanafi school.
الإباحة (al-ibahah): Permission to make use of something without owning it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the brother is not poor.
B) Because feeding is ibahah, not tamlik.
C) Because the court order prohibits it.
D) Because it is not a valid category.

Key Takeaway
Zakat requires tamlik; feeding a meal is ibahah and does not satisfy this condition according to Muhammad, but is accepted by Abu Yusuf and in later fatwa practice.

Giving Zakat to an Orphan: Clothing vs. Food
Ruling on clothing vs. feeding an orphan with zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on clothing vs. feeding an orphan with zakat, identify the condition for valid feeding (tamlik), and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Feeding an orphan with zakat requires ownership transfer; mere permission to eat is insufficient.

This passage addresses a specific application of zakat distribution: supporting an orphan. The Hanafi school distinguishes between clothing, which inherently transfers ownership, and feeding, which is merely a permission (ibahah) unless the food is given into the orphan's possession. There is a known intra-madhhab difference: Imam Muhammad restricts feeding, while Abu Yusuf allows both forms. The ruling hinges on the concept of tamlik (transfer of ownership), which is the essential characteristic of zakat disbursement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling for clothing
The passage begins: 'Whoever supports an orphan with clothing... it is permissible.' Note that clothing inherently involves transfer of ownership (tamlik), so it fulfills the requirement of zakat distribution.
Clothing is given directly to the orphan, making them owner.
2
Identify the act and the ruling for feeding
Feeding is not permissible by default because it is mere ibahah (permission to consume). Zakat requires tamlik – giving ownership of the property to the recipient.
Feeding without transfer of possession is not valid as zakat.
3
Recognize the exception for feeding
The text states: 'unless he gives it into his hand' – if the food is physically handed over to the orphan, making them its owner, then it becomes valid like clothing.
Possession (al-yad) creates ownership in Hanafi fiqh for fungible items.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf permits both clothing and feeding without the need for possession transfer. His view is based on a broader interpretation of maintenance (nafaqah) as a form of zakat.
The dominant (mufta bihi) position follows Imam Muhammad for precaution.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider: a man buys new clothes and gives them to an orphan vs. he invites the orphan to dinner. Only the former counts as zakat unless the food is handed over in ownership.
This distinction appears in modern zakat distribution where feeding programs must structure transactions with tamlik.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عال يتيماً بكسوة، وبنفقة من الزكاة: جاز في الكسوة، دون الإطعام؛ لأنه في الإطعام: إباحة، إلا أن يدفع إلى يده. وعن أبي يوسف: يجوز فيهما.
And whoever supports an orphan with clothing and with maintenance from zakat: it is permissible in clothing, but not in feeding; because in feeding it is a permission (ibahah), unless he gives it into his hand. And from Abu Yusuf: it is permissible in both.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage addresses giving zakat to support an orphan. Clothing is permissible as it involves transfer of ownership (tamlik). Feeding the orphan directly is not permissible as zakat because it is ibahah (permission to eat) unless the food is handed over to the orphan's possession (making it tamlik). Abu Yusuf disagrees and allows both, similar to his view in the previous fatwa. The majority view (Muhammad) restricts feeding unless possession is given.

Key Terms
دفع إلى يده (dafa'a ila yadihi): Giving into the orphan's hand, transferring possession.
إباحة (ibahah): Permission; allowing someone to use or consume something without transferring ownership.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; making the recipient the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Imam Muhammad), when is feeding an orphan with zakat valid?
A) Always, as long as the orphan is poor.
B) Only if the orphan is a relative.
C) Only if the food is handed over into the orphan's possession.
D) Never, because feeding is not zakat.

Key Takeaway
For zakat, giving an orphan clothing is always valid; feeding requires handing over the food to the orphan's possession to fulfill the condition of tamlik.

Giver Receives Food from Poor Recipient
Prohibition of giver benefiting from zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that a giver of zakat cannot receive benefit from the recipient unless through tamlik (ownership transfer).

Core Idea
Zakat requires complete transfer of ownership; any return of benefit invalidates it.

This short passage addresses a common scenario: after giving zakat to a poor person, the giver is offered food by that recipient. The ruling draws a sharp line between two types of transfer: tamlik (ownership) and ibahah (permission). Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring zakat is discharged validly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the giver receiving food from the poor recipient after giving zakat. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) in itself.
2
Distinguish between tamlik and ibahah
The passage states that receiving food is only allowed if it is given as tamlik (transfer of ownership). If it is merely ibahah (permission to eat without ownership), it is not allowed.
Tamlik means the poor person gives the food as a gift, making the giver the owner of that food. Ibahah is simply allowing the giver to eat without transferring ownership.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Zakat must leave the giver's ownership completely. Any return of benefit—even through the recipient’s invitation—effectively returns value to the giver, defeating the purpose of zakat.
This principle applies to zakat, ushr, and fitr (all charitable obligations).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل أعطى فقيراً من زكاته أو من عُشر أرضه، أو من فطرته، ثم إن الفقير أطعمه المعطي: لا يجوز ذلك، إلا على سبيل التمليك، ولا يجوز على سبيل الإباحة.
A man gave a poor person from his zakat or from the tithe of his land, or from his fitr, then the poor person fed the giver: that is not permissible, except on the basis of ownership (tamlik), and it is not permissible on the basis of permission (ibahah).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage prohibits a common scenario: after giving zakat to a poor person, the giver then receives food from that poor person. This is not allowed because it effectively returns the benefit to the giver, unless the poor person gives the food as a gift of ownership (tamlik). If the poor person simply invites the giver to eat (ibahah), it is null and does not count as valid zakat. The principle is that the zakat must leave the giver's ownership completely; any return of benefit invalidates it.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership, as opposed to ibahah (mere permission).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can the giver of zakat eat food that the poor recipient offers him?
A) Yes, always.
B) Yes, if it is a different food.
C) No, unless the food is given as ownership.
D) No, under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
Zakat requires complete divestment of ownership; any return of benefit, even as an invitation, invalidates it unless ownership is formally transferred.

Prohibition of Zakat to Bani Hashim: Consensus and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Prohibition of zakat to Bani Hashim with ijma proof and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to Bani Hashim, identify the ijma proof, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on Hashimi-to-Hashimi distribution.

Core Idea
Giving zakat to Bani Hashim by a non-Hashimi is haram by consensus; even among Hashimis, the majority prohibits it.

Zakat is a pillar of Islam with strict rules on recipients. One key prohibition is giving zakat to the clan of the Prophet, Bani Hashim. This lesson unpacks the evidence (dalil) and the internal Hanafi difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) on whether a Hashimi can give to another Hashimi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of giving zakat to Bani Hashim. The ruling is haram for a non-Hashimi. Note: this applies to any wajib sadaqah (zakat, sadqat al-fitr, nadhr, kaffarah).
The text explicitly states 'bil-ijma' meaning consensus.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites ijma (consensus) as the proof for the prohibition. Ijma is a qat'i (certain) source of evidence. There is no need for a specific Quranic verse or hadith when consensus is established.
The dalil is not quoted verbatim; the statement 'bil-ijma' suffices.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does ijma prove the ruling? Ijma of the scholars on this matter means it is a definitive (qat'i) prohibition. The commentator affirms this without qualification.
This is a strong proof; it cannot be overridden by istihsan.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text records a dispute among the Hanafi imams: 'When the two' (likely Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that a Hashimi cannot give zakat to another Hashimi, while Abu Yusuf permits it. This is an internal Hanafi khilaf.
The identity of 'the two' needs verification; it is generally Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position for fatwa is the prohibition for non-Hashimi (by ijma), and for Hashimi-to-Hashimi, the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is taken, making it also prohibited. Abu Yusuf's opinion is considered a weak (marjuh) position.
This is consistent with the majority of later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تُدفع إلى بني هاشم، وكذا لا يجوز لغني آخر، أو هاشمي، أو لأبي المعطي، أو لابنه إذا كان على سبيل الإباحة، ويجوز على سبيل التمليك. فإن تبدلت العين المعطاة بأن باعها الفقير بعين أخرى، بأن كان تمراً، فباعه بزبيب أو بحنطة أو ما أشبه ذلك : جاز فيها الإباحة، وتبدل العين كتبدل الملك.
* قوله: (ولا تدفع إلى بني هاشم) .
يعني الأجنبي لا يدفع إليهم، بالإجماع وهل يجوز أن يدفع بعضهم إلى بعض؟ - عندهما لا يجوز، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز. وأما التطوع فيجوز صَرْفُه إليهم؛ لأن المال في الزكاة: كالماء يتدنّس بإسقاط الفرض، والتطوع بمنزلة التبرد بالماء. وكذا يجوز صرف صدقة الأوقاف إليهم إذا سماهم الواقف في الوقف؛ لأنها ليست بغُسالة، إذ لم يسقط بها فرض. - وأما إذا لم يسمهم الواقف : فلا يجوز؛ لأنه إذا سماهم: كان حكم ذلك حكم التطوع، بدلالة أنه يجوز للواقف أن يشترطه للأغنياء، فكذا لبني هاشم. كذا في الكرخي». أما إذا أطلق الواقف : لم يجز؛ لأنه تكون صدقة واجبة. ويجوز صرف خُمس الركاز والمعدن إلى فقراء بني هاشم.
And it is not given to Bani Hashim, and similarly it is not permissible for another wealthy person, or a Hashimi, or the giver's father, or his son, if it is on the basis of ibahah (permission), and it is permissible on the basis of tamlik (ownership). Then if the given item is exchanged by the poor person selling it for another item, such as if it was dates and he sold it for raisins or wheat or the like, then ibahah is permissible in that case, and the exchange of the item is like the exchange of ownership.
* His statement: (And it is not given to Bani Hashim). It means a non-Hashimi does not give to them by consensus. And is it permissible for some of them to give to others? According to the two (imams) it is not permissible, but Abu Yusuf said it is permissible. As for voluntary (sadaqah), it is permissible to spend on them, because wealth in zakat is like water that becomes filthy by discharging the obligation, and voluntary is like cooling with water. Similarly, it is permissible to spend waqf sadaqah on them if the waqif named them in the waqf, because it is not like wash water, since no obligation is discharged by it. And if the waqif did not name them, it is not permissible; because when he names them, the ruling becomes that of voluntary sadaqah, as evidence that it is permissible for the waqif to stipulate it for the wealthy, so too for Bani Hashim. This is in al-Karkhi. But if the waqif is general (unrestricted), it is not permissible, because it becomes obligatory sadaqah. And it is permissible to spend the khumus of treasure and mine on the poor of Bani Hashim.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This extensive passage covers multiple rulings regarding the prohibition of giving zakat (and obligatory sadaqat) to Bani Hashim by non-Hashimis, by consensus (ijma). However, there is a dispute among the Hanafi imams whether a Hashimi can give zakat to another Hashimi: the two (likely Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, or Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Actually the text says 'عندهما لا يجوز، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز' so 'عندهما' must refer to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) forbid it, while Abu Yusuf permits it. Voluntary sadaqah (nafl) is allowed to them, likened to using water for cooling after using it for purification. Waqf sadaqah to Bani Hashim is allowed if the waqif specifically named them; otherwise it is treated as wajib sadaqah and thus prohibited. Finally, the khumus of rikaz (treasure) and ma'din (mines) may be given to poor Bani Hashim, because it is not like zakat in being 'wash water' but rather a separate due.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Bani Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم; they cannot receive zakat.
الإباحة (al-ibahah): Permission to use without ownership transfer.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the consensus (ijma) mentioned in the text, who is prohibited from giving zakat to Bani Hashim?
A) A Hashimi.
B) A non-Hashimi.
C) A wealthy person.
D) A poor person.

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat to Bani Hashim by a non-Hashimi is haram by consensus; among Hashimis, the majority prohibits it.

Exceptions: Voluntary Sadaqah, Waqf, Khumus, and the Ibahah/Tamlik Distinction
Exceptions to prohibition: voluntary, waqf, khumus, and the ibahah/tamlik rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which it is permissible to give sadaqah (voluntary, waqf, khumus) to Bani Hashim and how the ibahah/tamlik distinction affects zakat validity.

Core Idea
Bani Hashim can receive voluntary sadaqah, waqf (if named), and khumus; ibahah is invalid for zakat unless the item is exchanged.

The prohibition of zakat to Bani Hashim is strict, but there are notable exceptions. This lesson explores when it is permissible to give to Bani Hashim and clarifies the critical condition of ownership transfer (tamlik) versus mere permission (ibahah) in all zakat transactions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish wajib from voluntary sadaqah
The prohibition applies to wajib sadaqah (zakat, al-fitr, etc.). Voluntary sadaqah (nafl) is allowed to Bani Hashim. The text uses a metaphor: wajib sadaqah is like dirty water used for wudu; voluntary is like fresh water for cooling.
This is a key distinction: the 'dirtiness' of obligation prevents giving to the Prophet's family.
2
Analyze waqf sadaqah
If a waqif (founder of a charity endowment) specifically names Bani Hashim as beneficiaries, the sadaqah from that waqf is treated like voluntary sadaqah and is permissible. If the waqif does not specify, it is considered wajib and thus not allowed.
The evidence: it is permissible to stipulate waqf for the wealthy, so similarly for Bani Hashim when named.
3
Understand khumus of rikaz and mines
The one-fifth (khumus) of buried treasure and minerals is explicitly allowed to be given to the poor of Bani Hashim. This is because khumus is not a purifying sadaqah like zakat; it is a separate due.
Khumus here refers to the share from treasure and mines, not war booty.
4
Grasp the ibahah vs tamlik condition
Zakat must be given in tamlik (transfer of ownership). Ibahah (mere permission to use) does not constitute valid zakat. However, if the poor recipient sells the item and takes ownership of the price, then ibahah becomes effective because ownership has transferred.
This applies to all zakat, not just Bani Hashim.
5
Apply to scenarios
Now you can determine: a Hashimi can receive zakat from a non-Hashimi via a waqf if named, or via khumus. But direct zakat from a non-Hashimi is never allowed. Also ensure that any zakat given (to non-Hashimi) must be tamlik, not ibahah.
The exchange rule: if the item is later sold, ibahah becomes tamlik-like.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تُدفع إلى بني هاشم، وكذا لا يجوز لغني آخر، أو هاشمي، أو لأبي المعطي، أو لابنه إذا كان على سبيل الإباحة، ويجوز على سبيل التمليك. فإن تبدلت العين المعطاة بأن باعها الفقير بعين أخرى، بأن كان تمراً، فباعه بزبيب أو بحنطة أو ما أشبه ذلك : جاز فيها الإباحة، وتبدل العين كتبدل الملك.
* قوله: (ولا تدفع إلى بني هاشم) .
يعني الأجنبي لا يدفع إليهم، بالإجماع وهل يجوز أن يدفع بعضهم إلى بعض؟ - عندهما لا يجوز، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز. وأما التطوع فيجوز صَرْفُه إليهم؛ لأن المال في الزكاة: كالماء يتدنّس بإسقاط الفرض، والتطوع بمنزلة التبرد بالماء. وكذا يجوز صرف صدقة الأوقاف إليهم إذا سماهم الواقف في الوقف؛ لأنها ليست بغُسالة، إذ لم يسقط بها فرض. - وأما إذا لم يسمهم الواقف : فلا يجوز؛ لأنه إذا سماهم: كان حكم ذلك حكم التطوع، بدلالة أنه يجوز للواقف أن يشترطه للأغنياء، فكذا لبني هاشم. كذا في الكرخي». أما إذا أطلق الواقف : لم يجز؛ لأنه تكون صدقة واجبة. ويجوز صرف خُمس الركاز والمعدن إلى فقراء بني هاشم.

The Prohibition of Zakat to Banu Hashim and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on prohibition and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to Banu Hashim and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether Hashimis may give zakat to each other.

Core Idea
Zakat to Banu Hashim is prohibited for outsiders by consensus; the interior is disputed between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad (prohibited) and Abu Yusuf (permitted).

The prohibition of giving zakat to Banu Hashim is a well-established ruling across the madhahib. However, an important intra-Hanafi dispute arises concerning whether a Hashimi can give zakat to another Hashimi. This lesson explores the consensus on outsiders, the differing positions within the Hanafi school, and related rulings on voluntary charity and endowments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus ruling
The text states that an outsider (non-Hashimi) cannot give zakat to Banu Hashim by consensus. This is the basic ruling.
Consensus means all schools agree, so this is not contested.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two elders) prohibit a Hashimi from giving zakat to another Hashimi, while Abu Yusuf permits it. The phrase 'عندهما لا يجوز' indicates the majority view.
Abu Yusuf's argument is not detailed in the text.
3
Understand the basis of the dispute
The text uses an analogy: zakat is like water that becomes impure from washing off obligation, so it cannot be given within the prohibited group. Voluntary charity is like cooling water, remaining pure, thus permissible.
This analogy helps explain why voluntary charity is allowed for Banu Hashim.
4
Apply to related cases
Consider endowments: if the endower names Banu Hashim, it is permissible; if not, it is not, because an absolute endowment becomes obligatory sadaqah. Also, khums from rikaz and minerals can be given to poor Hashimis.
Endowment (waqf) is treated like voluntary charity when specific beneficiaries are named.
5
Define who is Banu Hashim
The families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, and their clients (mawali) are included. Descendants of Abu Lahab are not, as they did not support the Prophet.
This definition is crucial for applying the prohibition correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تدفع إلى بني هاشم،
باب من يجوز دفع الصدقة إليه، ومن لا يجوز
وكذا لا يجوز لغني آخر، أو هاشمي، أو لأبي المعطي، أو لابنه إذا كان على سبيل الإباحة، ويجوز على سبيل التمليك.
فإن تبدلت العين المعطاة بأن باعها الفقير بعين أخرى، بأن كان تمراً، فباعه بزبيب أو بحنطة أو ما أشبه ذلك : جاز فيها الإباحة، وتبدل العين كتبدل الملك.
* قوله: (ولا تدفع إلى بني هاشم) .
يعني الأجنبي لا يدفع إليهم، بالإجماع
وهل يجوز أن يدفع بعضهم إلى بعض؟
-
عندهما لا يجوز، وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز.
وأما التطوع فيجوز صَرْفُه إليهم؛ لأن المال في الزكاة: كالماء يتدنّس بإسقاط الفرض، والتطوع بمنزلة التبرد بالماء.
-
وكذا يجوز صرف صدقة الأوقاف إليهم إذا سماهم الواقف في الوقف؛ لأنها ليست بغُسالة، إذ لم يسقط بها فرض.
- وأما إذا لم يسمهم الواقف : فلا يجوز؛ لأنه إذا سماهم: كان حكم ذلك حكم التطوع، بدلالة أنه يجوز للواقف أن يشترطه للأغنياء، فكذا لبني هاشم. كذا في الكرخي».
أما إذا أطلق الواقف : لم يجز؛ لأنه تكون صدقة واجبة.
ويجوز صرف خُمس الركاز والمعدن إلى فقراء بني هاشم.
وهم : آل علي، وآل عباس، وآل جعفر ، وآل عقيل، وآل الحارث بن عبد المطلب، ومواليهم.
وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : إذا دفع الزكاة إلى رجل يظنُّه فقيراً، ثم بان أنه غني، أو هاشمي، أو كافر، أو دفع في ظلمة إلى فقير، ثم بان أنه أبوه، أو ابنه : فلا إعادة عليه .
- ولا يجوز لهم النذور والكفارات ولا صدقة الفطر، ولا جزاء الصيد ؛ لأنها صدقة واجبة. كذا عند أبي يوسف.
ولا يجوز لبني هاشم أن يعملوا على الصدقة؛ لأنها وإن كانت أجرة من وجه، فهي صدقة من وجه واستوى الحظر والإباحة: فغلب الحظر. قال أبو يوسف : إلا أن يكون رزقهم على العمل من غيرها: فيجوز.
* قوله: (وهم: آل علي، وآل العباس، وآل جعفر، وآل عقيل، وال الحارث بن عبد المطلب؛ لأن هؤلاء كلهم يُنسبون إلى هاشم بن عبد مناف. وفائدة التخصيص بهؤلاء : أنه يجوز الدفع إلى من عداهم من بني هاشم، كذرية أبي لهب ؛ لأنهم لم يناصروا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. * قوله: ( ومواليهم) أي عبيدهم؛ لأن مواليهم تشرفوا بشرفهم. وأما مكاتبوهم: فذَكَرَ في الوجيز» خلافاً، والظاهر منه أنه لا يجوز. قوله: (وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد: إذا دفع الزكاة إلى رجل يظنه فقيراً، ثم بان أنه غني، أو هاشمي، أو كافر، أو دفع في ظلمة إلى فقير، ثم بان أنه أبوه، أو ابنه: فلا إعادة عليه).
And it is not given to Banu Hashim. Chapter on who may be given sadaqah and who may not. Similarly, it is not permissible for another rich person, or a Hashimi, or for the father of the giver, or his son if it is by way of permissibility (ibaahah), but it is permissible by way of ownership (tamlik). If the given item is exchanged, such that the poor person sells it for another item, e.g., dates, and he sells it for raisins or wheat or the like, then permissibility is allowed therein, and the exchange of the item is like exchange of ownership. * His saying: (And it is not given to Banu Hashim). He means an outsider does not give to them, by consensus. And is it permissible for some of them to give to others? According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), it is not permissible, and Abu Yusuf said: it is permissible. As for voluntary charity, it is permissible to give it to them; because the wealth in zakat is like water that becomes dirty by discharging the obligation, and voluntary charity is like cooling oneself with water. Similarly, it is permissible to give the sadaqah of endowments to them if the endower named them in the endowment; because it is not like wash-water, since no obligation is discharged by it. And if the endower did not name them, then it is not permissible; because if he named them, its ruling is the ruling of voluntary charity, evidenced by the fact that the endower may stipulate it for the rich, so also for Banu Hashim. This is in al-Karkhi. But if the endower gave absolutely, it is not permissible, because it becomes obligatory sadaqah. And it is permissible to give the khums of rikaz and mines to the poor of Banu Hashim. They are: the family of Ali, the family of Abbas, the family of Ja'far, the family of Aqil, the family of al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, and their clients (mawali). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: If a person gives zakat to a man whom he thinks is poor, and then it becomes apparent that he is rich, or a Hashimi, or a disbeliever, or he gave in darkness to a poor person and then it becomes apparent that he is his father or his son, then there is no repetition upon him. And it is not permissible for them (Banu Hashim) to receive vows, expiations, zakah al-fitr, or the compensation for hunting, because they are obligatory sadaqah. This is according to Abu Yusuf. It is not permissible for Banu Hashim to work as collectors of sadaqah; because even though it is a wage in one respect, it is sadaqah in another, and prohibition and permissibility are equal, so prohibition prevails. Abu Yusuf said: Unless their sustenance is from other than that work, then it is permissible. * His saying: (And they are: the family of Ali, the family of Abbas, the family of Ja'far, the family of Aqil, and the family of al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib; because all of these are attributed to Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. The benefit of specifying these is that it is permissible to give to those besides them from Banu Hashim, like the descendants of Abu Lahab; because they did not support the Prophet, peace be upon him. * His saying: (and their clients) meaning their slaves; because their clients are honored by their honor. As for their mukatabs (slaves under contract of manumission): it is mentioned in al-Wajiz that there is a difference of opinion, and the apparent is that it is not permissible. His saying: (And Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: If a person gives zakat to a man whom he thinks is poor, and then it becomes apparent that he is rich, or a Hashimi, or a disbeliever, or he gave in darkness to a poor person and then it becomes apparent that he is his father or his son, then there is no repetition upon him).
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah on the chapter regarding who may and may not receive zakat. The author begins by stating the well-known ruling: zakat is not to be given to Banu Hashim (the clan of the Prophet). This is by consensus concerning outsiders (non-Hashimis) giving to them. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding whether some Hashimis may give zakat to other Hashimis: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, it is not permitted, while Abu Yusuf permits it. Voluntary charity (tatawwu') is allowed for Banu Hashim because it does not carry the same obligation as zakat. The author compares zakat money to water used for purification (which becomes impure after use) and voluntary charity to water used for cooling (which remains pure). For endowments (awqaf), if the endower specifically names Banu Hashim as beneficiaries, it is permissible; otherwise, it is not, because an absolute endowment is considered obligatory sadaqah. The khums (one-fifth) of rikaz (buried treasure) and minerals may be given to the poor of Banu Hashim according to the author. The passage then specifies which clans constitute Banu Hashim for this ruling: the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. Others claiming Hashimi lineage, such as the descendants of Abu Lahab, are not included because they did not support the Prophet. The clients (mawali) of Banu Hashim are included in the prohibition. The author then presents a key ruling from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: if a person gives zakat to someone he genuinely believes to be poor, but later discovers the recipient was actually rich, a Hashimi, a disbeliever, or in the case of giving in darkness to a poor person who turns out to be his father or son, the zakat is still valid and no repetition is required. This is because the giver fulfilled the obligation based on his best judgment (zann). Finally, Banu Hashim themselves are prohibited from receiving other obligatory sadaqat like vows, expiations, zakat al-fitr, and compensation for hunting (jazaa' al-sayd) according to Abu Yusuf, and they cannot work as zakat collectors except under certain conditions.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad, including the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqil, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib. They are prohibited from receiving zakat.
تطوع (tatawwu'): Voluntary charity, as opposed to obligatory zakat. It is permissible to give to Banu Hashim.
الموالي (al-mawali): Clients or freed slaves of Banu Hashim. They share the same ruling as Banu Hashim regarding prohibition of receiving zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is it permissible for a Hashimi to give his zakat to another Hashimi?
A) Yes, according to all Hanafi imams.
B) No, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; yes according to Abu Yusuf.
C) No, according to all Hanafi imams.
D) Yes, but only if the recipient is poor.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of zakat to Banu Hashim is clear for outsiders; the intra-Hashimi giving is disputed but the relied-upon view is prohibition.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
8 min
Mistaken Payment in Zakat: Giving to Someone Thought to be Eligible
Ruling on mistaken payment in zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that if a person gives zakat based on a genuine belief that the recipient is eligible, the zakat is valid even if it later turns out the recipient was ineligible.

Core Idea
Zakat given on a genuine belief of eligibility is valid even if the recipient proves ineligible—no repetition required.

When giving zakat, sometimes the giver relies on his best judgment about the recipient's eligibility. What happens if after payment the recipient is discovered to be rich, a Hashimi, a disbeliever, or even the giver's father or son? The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: the giver's obligation is discharged based on his apparent knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is giving zakat to someone thought to be poor. The condition is genuine belief (zann) based on appearance or knowledge.
The giver must have a reasonable belief; this is not applicable if the giver knowingly gave to an ineligible person.
2
State the ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if it later turns out the recipient was rich, a Hashimi, a disbeliever, or if gave in darkness to a poor person who turns out to be his father or son, the zakat is valid and no repetition is required.
The phrase 'فلا إعادة عليه' clearly indicates no necessity to repeat.
3
Understand the basis
The giver acted on his best judgment; the obligation is discharged because the condition at the time of giving was the giver's belief. The zakat is considered fulfilled.
This is based on the principle that actions are judged by intentions and apparent circumstances.
4
Distinguish from deliberate giving to ineligible
This ruling only applies when the giver did not know and had a genuine belief. If he knew the recipient was ineligible, the zakat would be invalid.
Knowledge of ineligibility invalidates the transaction.
5
Consider other cases from the text
The text also includes giving in darkness to a poor person who turns out to be his father or son—same ruling: no repetition.
Darkness implies difficulty in visual identification, reinforcing reliance on belief.

Key Terms
ظن (zann): Genuine belief or strong presumption based on available evidence.
لا إعادة عليه (la i'ada alayhi): No repetition is required upon him; the act is considered valid and fulfilled.

The Prohibition of Zakat for Banu Hashim and Their Mawali
Definition of prohibited recipients and rules on zakat collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which descendants of Banu Hashim are prohibited from receiving zakat and apply the ruling to related cases including mawali and mukatib.

Core Idea
Banu Hashim who aided the Prophet are honored by being prohibited from receiving zakah, but their mawali share this prohibition.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira defines the specific members of Banu Hashim who are prohibited from receiving zakat and other obligatory charities. It also addresses the status of their freed slaves (mawali) and the permissibility of working as zakat collectors, including an intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited groups
List the five families of Banu Hashim: Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, and their mawali.
Mawali are freed slaves who share the honor.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
Banu Hashim are prohibited from receiving obligatory charities because of their noble lineage; it preserves their dignity.
The prohibition applies to zakat, nadhr, kaffarat, fitr, and jaza' al-sayd.
3
Note the exception for descendants of Abu Lahab
Descendants of Abu Lahab are not prohibited because they did not support the Prophet.
This is the benefit of specifying the five families.
4
Recognize the ruling on working as collectors
Banu Hashim generally may not work as zakat collectors because the payment is considered charity. But Abu Yusuf permits it if their salary comes from non-zakat funds.
The default rule: prohibition prevails over permissibility.
5
Clarify the disagreement on mukatib
A mukatib (slave with manumission contract) of Banu Hashim: the apparent view is that he also cannot receive zakat, but there is difference in al-Wajiz.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب من يجوز دفع الصدقة إليه، ومن لا يجوز وهم : آل علي، وآل عباس، وآل جعفر ، وآل عقيل، وآل الحارث بن عبد المطلب، ومواليهم. ولا يجوز لهم النذور والكفارات ولا صدقة الفطر، ولا جزاء الصيد ؛ لأنها صدقة واجبة. كذا عند أبي يوسف. ولا يجوز لبني هاشم أن يعملوا على الصدقة؛ لأنها وإن كانت أجرة من وجه، فهي صدقة من وجه واستوى الحظر والإباحة: فغلب الحظر. قال أبو يوسف : إلا أن يكون رزقهم على العمل من غيرها: فيجوز. * قوله: (وهم: آل علي، وآل العباس، وآل جعفر، وآل عقيل، وال الحارث بن عبد المطلب؛ لأن هؤلاء كلهم يُنسبون إلى هاشم بن عبد مناف. وفائدة التخصيص بهؤلاء : أنه يجوز الدفع إلى من عداهم من بني هاشم، كذرية أبي لهب ؛ لأنهم لم يناصروا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. * قوله: ( ومواليهم) أي عبيدهم؛ لأن مواليهم تشرفوا بشرفهم. وأما مكاتبوهم: فذَكَرَ في الوجيز» خلافاً، والظاهر منه أنه لا يجوز.
Chapter: Who may be given charity and who may not. They are: the family of Ali, the family of Abbas, the family of Ja'far, the family of Aqeel, and the family of al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, and their freed slaves. It is not permissible for them to receive vows (nadhr), expiations (kaffarat), Fitr charity, or game compensation (jaza' al-sayd) because they are obligatory charity. This is the view of Abu Yusuf. It is not permissible for Banu Hashim to work as zakat collectors because although it is a wage in one sense, it is a charity in another sense; prohibition and permissibility are equal, so prohibition prevails. Abu Yusuf said: Unless their sustenance for the work is from other than the zakat, then it is permissible. * His statement: (They are: the family of Ali, the family of Abbas, the family of Ja'far, the family of Aqeel, and the family of al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib because all of them are attributed to Hashim ibn Abd Manaf. The benefit of specifying these is that it is permissible to give to other Banu Hashim, such as the descendants of Abu Lahab, because they did not aid the Prophet (peace be upon him). * His statement: (and their freed slaves) meaning their slaves because their freed slaves are honored by their honor. As for their mukatib slaves, there is a difference mentioned in al-Wajiz, and the apparent view is that it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It first defines which descendants of Hashim are prohibited from receiving zakat: the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, along with their freed slaves (mawali). The ruling also includes that these individuals cannot receive other types of obligatory charity such as vows, expiations, fitr charity, and game compensation, because all of these are considered zakat-like obligations. The commentator notes that this is the view of Abu Yusuf. Additionally, Banu Hashim are not allowed to work as zakat collectors because the payment they receive, while resembling a wage, is fundamentally a form of charity, and since both permissibility and prohibition are balanced, prohibition takes precedence. However, Abu Yusuf makes an exception: if they are given a separate salary not from the zakat funds, it is permissible. The commentary then explains the reason for specifying these particular families: they all trace lineage to Hashim ibn Abd Manaf, and the specification excludes other Banu Hashim like the descendants of Abu Lahab who did not support the Prophet, thus they may receive zakat. Regarding mawali, they are the freed slaves of these families; they are included in the prohibition because they share in the honor. For mukatib slaves (those who have a contract of manumission), there is a disagreement in al-Wajiz, but the apparent view is that they are not allowed to receive zakat either.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) including the families of Ali, Abbas, Ja'far, Aqeel, and al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib.
موالي (mawali): Freed slaves of Banu Hashim; they are also prohibited from receiving zakat due to their association.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract with his master to purchase his freedom; there is disagreement on whether he can receive zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is prohibited from receiving zakat?
A) A descendant of Abu Lahab
B) A freed slave (mawla) of Banu Hashim
C) A poor non-Hashimi Muslim
D) A mukatib slave of a non-Hashimi

Key Takeaway
Banu Hashim and their mawali are prohibited from receiving obligatory charity, but the prohibition is limited to the five families who supported the Prophet, excluding descendants of Abu Lahab.
